$=Antique Proto-Albanian,Standard Albanian
ˌɑ b ˌe r r ˈɑː n i oː , h a r ˈɔ j $verb: to forget. Orel 2000: 107. De Vaan 2017: 1745. 
ˌɑ b i ˈeː ɡ n u s , v ɟ ˈɛ $umbrella pine, black pine. Gheg: vgjê. Orel 2000: 110. Orel 1998: 506. Bonnet 161. For protests of this view see C̟abej 1960 (argues this is actually analogy-- reviewed in Bonnet p189) and Meyer 1891. 
ˌɑ b i ˈeː ɡ n e ɑ , v ɟ ˈɛ ɲ ə $regularly derived separate variant of the above per Bonnet 161, 190. -ɑ for feminine? 
ˌɑ b l ˌe w ˈɑː n i oː , b l ˈɛ j $verb: to buy. Çabej 1976 considers it native. Orel 2000: 36 argues its regular from Latin into PAlb, again later with regard to the -w-. Seems absent from Bonnet 1998. TODO Reexamine.
ˌɑ d i ˌuː n ˈɑː n i oː , a ɟ ə ɾ ˈɔ j $verb. To fast. Buzuku: engjënonj with prosthetic en-. Budiː angjinonj. Bardhiː me ngjinuem. Kuvendi i Berbenitː me anjënuem. Orel 1998: 2. 
ˌɑ d ˌoː r ˈɑː n i oː , a ð u ɾ ˈɔ j $Verb. To adore, worship. Orel 2000 thinks it may be a learned borrowing. Buzuku: adoronj, elsewhere in O Alb as adhëronj (-o- and -u- forms may have been influenced by Italian). Bonnet p168 supports this etymology but lists also adhëroj as a reflex. Supported also by De Vaan 2017: 1745, though notes it "may have been borrowed from a later variety of Romance". 
ˈɑː eː r , ˈɛ ɾ ə $erë, meaning wind, smell. Some argue it is native. Or a later borrowing from Romance -- cf Orel 1998: 89. Bonnet p135 argues for a loan from Greek aéra instead. Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˈɑː eː r , ˈa j ə ɾ $air. Some argue it is a learned loan from Latin, or Italian aria (i.e. Topalli 2017). Bonnet p135 argues it is native, and erë is not. 
ˌa e̯ r ˈɑː m e n , r ˈɛ m b $bronze, copper. Bonnet p118, 296. Also as rrëmb. In Gheg: rrem. Vatasescu p140: Old Alb rembje,  ram is an italian loan. Romanian aramă. Form in Newmark 1998(:744) is rremb, of which rrem and rrëmb are listed as variants (Newmark 1998: 744, 747).
ˌa e̯ s t i m ˈɑː n i oː , t͡ʃ m ˈɔ j $verb: to estimate, appreciate, esteem, value. From Latin per Orel 2000(:119), while Demiraj 1997 asserts a native PAlb *tHmō (what is this in other notations?).  Reexamine. Bonnet p375 supports. De Vaan 2017: 1742 as shmoj > çmoj. Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˈa e̯ t ɑː t e m , j ˈɛ t ə $life(time). Orel 1998 uses this to posit a native PAlb *eta, but it has also been attributed to Latin, albeit with the continuation of some old Indo-European semantic patterns that were likely inherited -- cf. Hamp 1968 and Joseph 2006 and 2022. Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˌɑː k ˈi n ɡ u l u m , c ˈɛ ɲ ɟ $lamb. Masculine. Plural qengja, or suppletion (?) with shqerra, shtjerra. Cham: qingj (?! not kingl-?!), Tosk variant qingjth, Gheg qîgj/kîgj, kingj. Attributed to a dialect loan from Cham by some. acingla is some double metathesis of agniiculus, diminutive of agnus "lamb". May need to re-examine. Orel 1998: 356. Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˌɑ k k ˈi p i t u m , c ˈi f t $kite, kind of vulture. Orel 2000: 118. Orel 1998: 360. From accipiter but -er lost like imperator > mbret, see Orel 1998(:360). Bonnet p36 attributes it to accípitrem, but posits on p38 the actiohn of a "croisement" (which he uses for cross-linguistic blends) with Byzantine Greek oskýpteros. He also argues (p267) it was "sans doute" an Old Albanian *qiftër, that is tested below. (case of skifter -- speculation I've seen is that it is perhaps some blend of a sk- hawk like prefix, cf shkabë, with this original qiftër) De Vaan 2017: 1735 -- as "red kite".
ˌɑ k k ˈi p i t r e m , c ˈi f t ə ɾ $kite. See above. Testing hypothesis of Bonnet. Matasovic 2024: 61 as accipiter. 
ˌɑ k s ˈu n ɡ i ɑ , u ʃ ˈu ɲ ə $lard. Gheg: ushûj, Tosk ushu(n)jë Arbëreshë nxunxë, Arvanitika ashung. Orel 2000: 114. Bonnet p375 (using ushunj) argues ushunj form was produced under the influence of Byzantine Greek oksoungi as per Bonnet 120-121. See also Bonnet 191, 264. For morphological paradigmatic influence of Greek, see Bonnet 290. Arbereshe has nxunxë -- Giordani 1963 apud Vatasescu 1997 p88. Vatasecsu 1997(:88) conversely argues it is Arvanitika ashung that is influenced by Greek, following Mihaescu 1981(:221); Romanian osânză.  
ˈɑ l b ɑ n u m , ˈa ɾ b ə ɾ $Albanian. Orel 1998: 7. Gheg: arbën. Bonnet p325 howeverː "le base Arbën... est sans doute indigène. Meyer 1891, p.14, avait relevé que le nom de la Labëri en offre une variante à metathèse et dissimilation (postérieure au rhotacism ǃ )". 
ˌɑ l b ɑː n ˈe n s i s , a ɾ b ə ɾ ˈɛ ʃ $Albanian. Gheg: arbënesh. Bonnet p325,375 
ˈɑ l i u m , ˈa j $bite (noun). Orel 1998: 3. 
ˌɑ l l ˌeː ɡ o r ˈiː ɑ , l ɔ ɡ ɔ ɾ ˈi $wailing, dirge. Attributed by Orel to "the earliest stages of Albanian contcts with Romance", the "oldest layer demonstrating ... Lat ē > Alb o, similar to the change of EPA *ē"...  reexamine.  Orel 1998(ː 230) -- earlier lëgori effected by assimilation.
ˌɑ l t ˈɑː r i u m , l t ˈɛ ɾ $altar. Orel 2000: 30. Orel 1998: 232. "elter" in De Vaan 2017: 1737. Bonnet p122 notes variantː ëlter (< altāria inline, though "altāre" in index). Vatasescu 1997(:478) lists modern Gheg lter and Old Alb (Gheg-- Buzuku) ëlter as corresponding to Romanian altar, both from altarivm. Matasovic 2024: 61 as altare(m?) > lter, elter. 
ˌɑ l t e r ˈɑː n i oː , n d ə r ˈɔ j $verb: to change, alter. Orel 2000: 58. Native etymology possible from PALb anteros < PIE *an-2, cf anë "side", andej "other side", ndryshoj, etc... supported by Orel 1998: 285 and Bonnet p275 (+375) too. 
ˌɑ l w ˈe o l u m , n ˈu lˠ ə $gum. Orel 2000: 111. Orel 1998(: 302) says this was borrowed in "its later meaning as a word for dental alveole. The Albanian form implies an earlier *lullë with dissimilation of sonorants" (also supported in Matasovic 2024: 61.). Alas this word does not seem to be in Bonnet 1998 or in Vatasescu 1997.
ˌɑ m ˈɑː r ɑ k iː , m a ɾ ˈa c $fennel. Alternate forms: maraj, mërajë. One or more of these may be from V  Latin marathri(c)um. Cf Romanian mărar. May be via Slavic or Byzantine Greek. Orel 1998: 244-5; Topalli 2017:938. TODO review stress placement on this? 
ɑ m b i p ˌɑ t ˈiː n u m , m t ˈi $churn (noun). Gheg: mtî. other variants: bëti, pëtî, muti, tpî. Orel attributes to Romance *patīna. AmbipatinU seems right here though, as here the "around" semantics are around? Re-examine? Orel 1998: 276.  
ˌɑ m b u l ˈɑː n i oː , m b a lˠ ˈɔ j $verb: to turn out to graise (>  mballesë "meadow, pasture"). From earlier *mbëlloj. Orel 1998: 248. 
ˌɑ m ˈɑ n d o l ɑ , m ˈɛ n d u lˠ $almond. Feminine. Orel 1998: 256. Or from amygdala (Vatasescu 166), or direct from Old Greek amygdala/amugdala (Cabej 1964); the two cited sources both find an Italian loan unlikely. AMYGDALA need not be the form cited -- the REW (number 436, p30) cotes attested amyndala as a headword as well as an *amandola, and -nd- or regularly reflected -nn- forms are seen in Sicilian, Tarentine, Neapolitan, Abruzzese, Logudorese, Portuguese, French (al(e)mande via metathesis > English almond); Romanian migdală is from Greek per the REW. Going with the Romance form 3 from the REW (may consider adding the #2, amyndala).
ˈɑ m i ɑ , ˈa m ɛ $brown meagre, a fish. Topalli 2017: 96. Bonnet 161, 375. Vatasescu 1997: 216. 
ˌɑ m ˈiː k u m , m ˈi k $friend. Orel 2000: 34. Bonnet 375. De Vaan 2017: 1735. Matasovic 2024: 61. Listed as -us for many of these. 
ˈɑ m i t ɑ m , ˈɛ m t ə $paternal aunt. Bonnet 375. De Vaan 2017: 1737. Vatasescu 1997 p105 -- via an emëtë. Demiraj 1989 p203. 
ˌɑ m ˈu r ɡ ɑ , m ˈu ɾ ɡ ə $lees of oil. Orel 1998: 278.  
ˈɑ n ɡ e l u m , ˈə ɲ ɟ ə lˠ $angel. Variant form exists with /ɛnɟ/ rather than /ənɟ/. Also variantː (Gheg?) ênjill per Bonnet p375. Also ungj-? May be inherited from plural? -- Cf Orel 2000 p24. Bonnet p115, 375. Vatasescu 1997: 448. Testing the form with /ɛ/ below.
ˈɑ n ɡ e l u m , ˈɛ ɲ ɟ ə lˠ $angel. See above. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˌɑ n ɡ u ˈi l l ɑ , ɲ ɟ ˈɛ l ə $eel. Orel 1998: 298. But may actually be native from Proto-Albanian *engellā per Topalli 2017(:1060), (cf Illyrian engella-- where did this come from? citation?). Reexamine. Note dialectal form njalë. Orel 1998(ː305) claims njilë "tench" is a dialectal variant of this same lexeme, but Bonnet p150 has them as two separate lexemes.  
ˌɑ n ɡ u ˈi l l ɑ , ɲ ɟ ˈa l ə $eel. See above (ngjelë); however this is the citation form in Newmark 1998(:589), and in Vatasescu 1997: 216. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˌɑ n ɡ u ˈi l l ɑ , ɲ ˈi l ə $eel. See above (ngjalë), and Bonnet p150/186/187/375, Orel 1998:305). De Vaan 2017: 1740.
ˌɑ n ɡ ˈu s t u s , ŋ ɡ ˈu ʃ t ə $narrow. Final schwa is because derived from angusta or because it's an adjective hence analogy. Orel 1998: 298. Bonnet 84/186/375; 84 on /u/ > Alb /u/.  +Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˌɑ n ɡ u s t ˈɑː n i oː , ŋ ɡ u ʃ t ˈɔ j $verb: to narrow. May be later formation based on ngushtë. Bonnet p351 argues it is a derivation internal to Albanian -- the phonetics are plausible for him but he makes this argument based on the low frequency of the angustāre in late Latin. 
ˌɑ n t ˈoː n i u s , n d ˈu ɛ $Anthony. Nickname: Ndoc (<Antonius > Antonts??) . Enduo in Buzuku. Bonnet 375. As Nduo in De Vaan 2017: 1737.  Perhaps from Antonus? TODO -- Ndua? 
ˌɑ n ˈu l l ɑ m , n ˈu l ə $paternal grandmother. Orel 1998: 302. 
ˌɑ p p ˌɑ r ɑ m ˈe n t u m , p a ɾ m ˈɛ n d ə $wooden plough. Change in gender may be reason for final ə. Gheg however has: parmênd.  Orel prefers peraramentum (not a typo) -- see Orel 1998: 311. Çabej as paramentum / apparamentum. Bonnet 339, 375 as parmënd... But this is the citation form used in Newmark 1998(: 623). 
ˌɑ p r ˈiː l i s , p ɾ ˈi lˠ $April. Orel 1998: 345. Bonnet 375. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˈɑ p t u m , ˈa f t ə $Final ë may be analogy as it is an adj. skilled, proficient. Fem sg is e afta. Demiraj 1997 thinks its native, but Orel 1998 and Topalli 2017 and Schumacher 2013 argue for Latin. Orel 2000: 118. Bonnet 375. De Vaan 2017: 1735 -- as from aptus (+Matasovic 2024: 61.).
ˌɑ r ˈeː n ɑ m , ɾ ˈə ɾ ə $sand. Orel 2000: 56. Bonnet 375. Gheg: rânë. De Vaan 2017: 1736. Rom arină. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˌɑ r ɡ ˈe n t u m , ə ɾ ɟ ˈə n d $silver. Was rgjand in Old Albanian, argjand in some Gheg. De Vaan 2017: 1736 -- for Gheg argjând.  Form "argjend" currently excluded due to contamination from Italian argento. Gheg: argjand. Orel 1998:8.  Bonnet 1998(:115) cites argjând as apparently Gheg, citing (p.116) ërgjënd for Tosk, while listing "argjând, ërgjent" on p120, while on p143, listing "rrgjând, ërgjënt". Orel 1998(: 89) uses ergjend -- is the lack of schwas simply a typo? Vătășescu 1997(:141) lists Old Albanian r̩gjand, Gheg rgjand, Tosk rgjënd. +Matasovic 2024: 61 as argjend. 
ˈɑ r m ɑ , ˈa ɾ m ə $weapon, arms. Orel 2000: 58, Orel 1998: 9, Topalli 2017: 121. Later loan from Italian arma also plausible. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˌɑ r m ˈɑː n i oː , a ɾ m ˈɔ j $verb: to take up arms. Only in Old Gheg (Bardhi) as me armuem. Cabej 1976 p81 apud Vatasescu 1997 p370. TODO move to Gheg cascade when possible. 
ˌɑ r m ˈɑː t ɑ , r ə m ˈa t ə $fleet. In Old Albanian, rmatë (in Budi) -- Vatasescu 1997: 369. Kristophson 1988(: 85) however thinks it is a loan from Italian. 
ˌɑ r m ˌi s s ˈɑː r i u m , h a ɾ m ə ʃ ˈu a̯ ɾ $stallion. Now Tosk harmëshor, but this is likely analogical (Old(?) Tosk harmëshuar cited in Puscariu apud Vatasescu). Varies with hamshor dialectally. Orel 2000(ː107) has it from admissārius. Vătășescu 1997(: 197) notes also Gheg harmshuer, and per Pușcariu, harmëshuar (Tosk?). 
ˌɑ r r ˌiː w ˈɑː n i oː , a r ˈi j $verb. To arrive. Orel 2000: 36. 
ˈɑ r k u m , h ˈa ɾ k $bow, arch. Orel 2000: 107. De Vaan 2017: 1745 as "curve".
ˈɑ r k u m , ˈa ɾ k $bow. Orel 2000: 106. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˈɑ r k ɑ m , ˈa ɾ k ə $chest, safe. Orel 1998: 9, Topalli 2017: 120. +Matasovic 2024: 61 for arqe. 
ˈɑ s k l ɑ , ˈa ʃ k ə l $splinter. Variant forms ashke, ashkë, ashkël, verb ashkloj. Old Albanian had ashklë and ashkje in Bogdani. Vatasescu 1997: 390. Romanian așchie. 
ˌɑ s p ˈɑ r ɑ ɡ u m , ʃ p ˈa ɾ ɡ $asparagus. Topalli 2017 (: 1407). In Vatasescu 1997(: 174) lists shpargull "sharp-leaved asparagus", which is in fact a separate word (Newmark 1998: 824), likely a diminuitivized derivation of the former. 
ˈɑ s p e r , ˈa ʃ p ə ɾ $rough. Orel 1998: 11. Bonnet 376. 
ˌɑː w ˈe n t u m , v ˈɛ n d $place. Uncertain new etymology per Matasovic 2024: 61.
ˌɑ w l ɑ m ˈe n t u m , a v l ə m ˈɛ n d $loom. Bonnet 1998(:339-40), Haarmann 1972. Per Bonnet 1998, the variant form avlimend is likely influenced by Greek avli. 
ˌɑ w ɡ ˈu s t u s , ɡ ˈu ʃ t $August. Orel 1998: 128. Bonnet 375. De Vaan 2017: 1735.
ˌɑ w r ˈe o l u m , r ˈy e̯ lˠ $euphorbia, a type of spurge. From an * āliōlum for Orel 1998(:391-2), for whihc he attributes Romanian alior "milkweed". However Sejdiu 1984(:104-5, apud Vatasecsu 1997: 179) attributes it to aureolus, which is also the accepted source of Romanian alior, with some sonorant switching. The etymology Orel uses is also disputed by Cabej 1986 II: 104. 
ˈɑ w r u m , ˈa ɾ $gold. Orel 2000: 35. But may need inspection as to why it is not *arr? De Vaan 2017: 1735 -- supports. LIkewise Schumacher & Matzinger 2013: 212. 
ˌɑ w ˈu n k u l u s , ˈu ɲ ɟ $uncle. Old Albanian ungël. Orel 1998: 487. De Vaan 2017: 1743. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
b ˈɑ l b iː , b ˈɛ l b ə $stammering, dumb. Per Orel 2000 p24: the regular development is only in the plural here. The final schwa may not be inherited. Bonnet 376/44 lists inherited forms as belbët, belbër but states that an adjectival -ër etymologically added (analog.: shurdh-ër, verb-ër etc). On -ët for belbët (and verbët) -- Bonnet 340. 
b ˈɑ l s ɑ m u m , b ˈa l t͡ʃ ə m $balm. Orel 2000: 103. Variantː balshëm -- unclear which is regular. Vatasescu 1997: 448. 
b ˈɑ n e ɑ , b ˈa ɲ ə $bath. Orel 1998(:16) attributes to Slavic, Çabej to Romance. 
b ˌɑ r ˈɑ tʰ r u m , b a l ˈa d ə ɾ $ballandër in Gheg. "Chasm". Etym per Topalli 2017, but may to change this one, but supported in Bonnet (368/376) as well. 
b ˌɑ r b ˈɑː t u m , v a ɾ v ˈa t $uncastrated (of horses and cows). Vatasescu 1997: 190. 
b ˈɑ r k ɑ m , b ˈa ɾ k ə $boat. Orel 2000: 24. Orel 1998: 18.
b ˌɑ r ˈoː n e m , b ə ɾ ˈu a̯ $shepherd. Actually this Tosk form doesn't occur -- this exists as bëruo in Old Albanian (Buzuku), and survives as brue in some Gheg dialects. Bonnet 1998: 367. 
b ˌɑ r r ˈiː n i oː , b a r i ɾ ˈɔ j $verb: to roar, bellow. Mann 1948(:21), Haarmann 1972(: 67), Meyer 1891(:28), Vatasescu 1998(:196), but otherwise might be onomatopoeia (Çabej 1976: 174 apud Vatasescu). This one actually from the infinitive barrīre-oniō? 
b ˌɑ t t u ˈɑː l i ɑ m , b ɛ t ˈɛ j ə $battle (Newmark 1998: 59). This etymology is not in Bonnet 1998 or Orel 1998 though it can be found online, and the word is also discussed as participating in a certain phraseological calque of Italian, but not listed as an Italian loan, by Dashi 2017(:20). This is essentially here a weak proposal -- though possible loaning instead from Romance other than Italian here may warrant consideration (although there does not seem to be a likely source). 
b ˌɑ t t u ˈɑː l i ɑ m , b ə t ˈa j ə $dread, terror; row, din, commotion; epilepsy (euphemism) (Newmark 1998: 75, marked as "regional"). Per Bonnet 1998(: 133) -- word is found in Mirdita (as bëtajë "disaster") and in the extreme south (Cham bëtalë); Bonnet dismisses a connection to Romanian bătaie on the basis of the Cham form's phonology, and hesitates between Latin battuālia, Latin battālia, and Italian battaglia; however Dashi 2017 does not include this word (while he does include "kali i betejës" as a calque of Italian cavallo di battaglia -- p20). On page 314, Bonnet 1998 seems to favor an Italian etymology for this however, with a semantic shift. May need to reconsider, and also in light of the connection (or lack thereof) to betejë.
b ˌe n e d iː k ˈo n i oː , b ə n k ˈɔ j $verb. To bless.  Orel 2000: 116. Orel 1998: 20 -- more conservative variant in bënkoj, but more common form in bekoj. benedīcere as root per Orel 2000 and 1998. Listed as "benedicāre" deriving a bēkoj (as bēkon) in De Vaan 1734, but as -īcere elsewhere -- De Vaan's derivation looks more convincing though, perhaps reexamine. Stated  as "irrégulière" in Bonnet 1999ː 354. Topalli 2017: 190-191. "bënkoj" considered to be regular, with bekoj arising from influence from "be" oath. Bonnet 1999(ː354) suspects influence also from mallkoj ("to curse"), arguing -ng- was expected second syllable onset, with the bene-male distinction beting familiar due to knowledge of Italian or medieval Latin historically. Already bekuem in Old Gheg (Buzuku) -- Vatasescu 1997: 478.
b ˈeː s t i ɑ m , b ˈi ʃ ə $wild animal, beast. Orel 2000:31-32, 103, 110. De Vaan 2017: 1743 as bēstia. Vatasescu 1997(:190) notes Romance *bīstia in Italian biscia and the (although she doesn't note it, irregular...?) French biche; ironically these could actually be the sources of Albanian bishë as a loan though (also Old French bisse, Venetian bisa)... nevertheless, traditional Latin supported apparently by Landi p119, Cabej 1986 II 246-7, Haarmann 112, MiHaescu, Meyer 1891ː38, apud Orel 1998:27. 
b ˌiː n ˈɑː r i u m , b i ɲ ˈa ɾ $twin. Variants: bin-ak, binjak, bin-osh. Tosk. Vatasescu 1997 p106. 
b l ˈe n n u m , b l ˈi $sturgeon. May be from Latin or AGk blennos or a native cognate, but the form is quite similar in all three cases. Meyer 1891: 40, Mihaescu 1993: 88, Cabej 1976: 264-5, apud Vatasescu 1997: 216. 
b ˈo l ɑ , b ˈɔ lˠ ə $harmless snake. Orel 2000: 27. Alternatively from bēlwa but that may also be Latin cf Italian belva.
b r ˈɑː k a e̯ , b ɾ ˈɛ k ə $knickers, underwear. Cf Romanian brace. Inherited form is from plural -- Orel 2000 p24. Bonnet 376 as from brāca. 
b r ˈo k k ɑ , p ɾ ˈɔ k ə $nail, fork. "Unusual voicing in anlaut" -- Orel 1998: 347. 
b r ˈoː t ɑ k u s , b ɾ ˈɛ t k $frog. Source form per Orel 2000: 28; brōt-h-acus in Vatasescu 1997(: 219); Romanian broatec. Old Albanian and Arvanitika bretëk, derived bretk-osë (Vatasescu p219). 
b r ˈoː t ɑ s k u s , b ɾ ˈɛ ʃ k ə $tortoise. Source form per Orel 2000: 28. 
b r ˈoː s k ɑ m , b ɾ ˈɛ ʃ k ə $turtle frog. Per Vatasescu 1997: 219. Rom broasca, Arom broasca. 
b r ˈuː m ɑ m , b ɾ ˈy m ə $rime, frost. Assigned as an "early loan" because it evinces Lat ū > Alb y by Orel 2000(ː34). +Bonnet 88, 376.  De Vaan 2017: 1736 -- as "hoar-frost". 
b ˈu b ɑ l u s , b ˈu a̯ lˠ $buffalo. De Vaan 2017: 1745; Bonnet 376. Diachrony  discussion -- see Bonnet 91-92, 199. Old Albanian buoll -- Cabej 1976 339 apud Vatasescu 1997 p206. Gheg buell it seems per Orel 1998:39. So PAlb o or ō? 
b ˌu b u l k ˈeː n s i s , b u l t͡ʃ ˈɛ ʃ $toponym, near Tirana. From same base as bujk below. Bonnet 1998: 325.
b ˌu b ˈu l k u m , b ˈu j k $farmer. Orel 2000: 113. Variant bulk. It may be the plural that is regularly inheritedː bulq -- see Bonnet 252. Derivations include bulqinë (Kuvendi i Arbënit - 1712), cf Cabej 1976. 
b ˈu l l ɑ m , b ˈu l ə $bud. Orel 2000: 29; Bonnet 376. Slender l is probably because we had a geminate in Latin, not a loan from OF boule or OO bola /bula/.
b ˈu k k ɑ m , b ˈu k ə $bread, meal. Orel 2000(:103) attributes this to Latin, semantics argued for in Orel 1998: 39-40. But could also be from a PAlb *bukā per Pokorny 1959ː 162. Support for Latin : De Vaan 2017: 1743, Bonnet 1999: 376.
b ˈu k s u m , b ˈu ʃ $boxwood. Orel 2000: 118. Bonnet 376 as buxus. But may actually be from a Late Latin *bussus -- see Bonnet 263-5. 
b ˈu r ɡ u s , b ˈu ɾ ɡ $prison, barn. Bonnet 376/369, also Miklosich. Orel 2000:42 attributes to "Gmc *burg-" cf Goth baurgs, OHG burg; not from Greek pýrgos. Bonnet does not dispute the ultimate Latin origin but seems to argue it came to Albanian through Latin. 
b ˈu t t e m , b ˈu t $big barrel. Orel 2000: 29, 118. Vatasescu 1998: 21, Haarman 1972: 49. 
d ˈa e̯ m o n e m , d j ˈɛ m ə ɾ $djemër does not actually occur; this word is only reflected as djemën among specifically Gheg Catholics. Orel 1998: 68.
d ˌɑ m n ˈɑː n i oː , d ə n ˈɔ j $verb: to punish. Old Gheg, in Budi: dëmënim (for the verbal noun). Vatasescu 1997: 421. =Romanian a dăuna? 
d ˈɑ m n u m , d ˈə m $harm, detriment (noun). Orel 2000: 105. Gheg: dam (dām). Bonnet 378. De Vaan 2017: 1736 as dām meaning "damage". Vatasescu 421 ~ Gheg dâm. Romanian daună.
d ˌeː b i t ˈɑː n i oː , d ə t ˈɔ j $to be obligated, an Arbereshe item, related to Romanian a datora. Vatasescu 1997: 442.

d ˌi ɡ i t ˈɑː n i oː , d ə f t ˈɔ j $verb: to show. Variants: dëftej (Korça? Meyer 1891); diftoj (Bonnet 259 -- slightly different diachrony and semantics per Bonnet 261?). d ˌiː r ˈeː k t u m , d ɾ ˈɛ j t $adj, direct, straight, right, just, correct. Orel 1998: 73; but Orel 1998(:17) also posits it as a lone from "Dalmatian (i.e. Western) Romance" along with trajtë and pajtoj. May be from V Latin drectus like Old French droit etc. De Vaan 2017: 1745 as drejt, no final ë. Bonnet 1998 has drejt and drejtë as separate etyma from the same source; Bonnet pp260, 348 on relation to dëreq, ndreq, dreqtë -- dëreq and drejtë attested in Buzuku, adverb dreq in Cham, Gjirokaster, Arbereshe, Arvanitika; Cabej 1958 (cited in Bonnet 1998: 260) attributes this to a local q/j alteration in the south. Treating the final -ë̈as orthographic here as it is not sounded in the standard and may function to signal the -a fpl form of the adjective. Comparison to Old Romanian derept -- Vatasescu 1997(:35), Cabej 1987: 312.
d i s , z $prefix . "to, dis, be-". Alternate forms: zh-. Not to be confused with the other prefix zh- which is a variant of sh-, native, "de-"; some z- from the Albanian inherited cognate of PIE *dis- also reflect this way. Orel 2000: 172-3.
d ˌi s k ˌɑ b ɑ l l i k ˈɑː n i oː , ʃ k a l k ˈɔ j $verb: to dismount. Vatasescu 1997: 330. 
d ˌi s k ˌɑ r r i k ˈɑː n i oː , ʃ k a ɾ k ˈɔ j $verb: to discharge, emit, unload, download. Suggestion from me: this may actually reflect excarricare, even though we have Romanian descărca. Orel 1998: 417. 
d ˈi s k u m , d ˈi ʃ k ə $mill funnel. earlier form dishkë, doublet of dhisk, later form dishtë. We are adhering to the view that dhisk is from Greek dhiskos, via medieval Greek. Orel 2000: 28, 105. Variant dishtë. 
d ˌi s k w ˌi r i t ˈɑː n i oː , ʃ c y ɾ t ˈɔ j $verb: to examine, observe. Meyer has it from Romance *disquiritare, as does Vatasescu 1997(:66), noting Old Gheg çqyrëtinj. Cabej derives it from qyr. Orel argues for an original unattested shkrytoj. Orel 1998: 435. 
d ˌi s s p ˌiː r ˈɑː n i oː , ʃ p i r ˈɔ j $form unattested, but source of deverbal shpirrë "asthma". Orel 1998: 430. 
m ˌo d e s t i k ˈɑː n i oː , m u ʃ t ə k ˈɔ j $verb: to adapt, adjust, acclimatize. Metathesized from domestic- (per Orel?). Now only mediopassive mushtekohem. Orel 1998: 280.
d ˌo l ˈeː n i oː , d ə lˠ ˈɛ j $ Now as përdëllej "to pity", per Çabej 1986(2:5-26) and Bonnet 1998(: 253, 349, 378). However La Piana and Mihaescu attribute it to Latin perdolēre "to grieve greatly", while Orel 1998 and Meyer attribute it to a Romance *per-indulgere, which Bonnet 1998 explicitly rejects. Orel 1998 lists it as përdëlej.   
d ˌo l ˈoː r e m , d u lˠ ˈy e̯ $ pain (= Romanian duroare). Old Albanian, att. in Buzuku "bukënë e dullyesë" but cf modern Gheg dellë "mercy, compassion" per Çabej 1982 (:355). Vatasescu 1997: 54. 
d ˌoː n ˈɑː n i oː , ð u ɾ ˈɔ j $verb, to bestow or give. Some argue it is native. Orel 2000: 33. Orel 1998: 84. Bonnet 378. Bonnet (1998: 181-2) attributes the Gheg form dhuroj to a Tosk dialect loan, motivated in part by a desire to distinguish this from dhunoj "to violate" (< dhunë "violence"). 
d r ˈɑ k iː , d ɾ ˈɛ c $dreq -- "devil", also as interjection "damn". From "singularized plural" of draco:; or just inherited from plural as per Orel 2000 p24. Bonnet 378. 
d r ˌɑ n k ˈoː n e m , d ɾ a ɡ ˈu a̯ $dragon, hero, or dragon slayer. Schumacher and Matzinger argue its from a Vulgar Latin drago:nem, not draco:nem. def nom sg and indef gen/dat/abl sg as dragoi. Pl dragonj, dragonjtë. Based on Gheg drangue, Orel 2000(:33) convincingly argues the source was not Latin dracōnem but rather drancōnem. De Vaan 2017: 1736 as drangua/drangue. 
d ˈu k e m , d ˈu c $spigot, tap, bung, barrel of a gun, conduit. Attributed to ductum by Meyer. Orel 1998: 78, Çabej 1986(148). Bonnet 1998 (p379 as dux, but ducem on pp85, 260). Orel 1998 notes similar semantic developments in Romance languages, Bonnet 1998 notes Dalmatian dauk "gutter" and some parallel developments in Gallo-Romance, i.e. Old French doiz "canal, conduit d'eau" (FEW 3: 195), etc. 
d ˌu p l i k ˈɑː n i oː , d u l p ə k ˈɔ j $verb: to double. Gheg dylpëkoj, Romanian a dupleca. Vatasescu 1997: 311 -- the semantics of the Albanian are closer to Romanian (and Latin) than the Western Romance forms such as doppiare; Vatasescu attributes Gheg -y- to the influence of Albanian dy "two". Arvanite dulpëk "double" (possibly from dulpicem??)
d ˌuː r ˈɑː n i oː , d u ɾ ˈɔ j $verb: to endure, tolerate, persist. Orel 2000(:34) considers a "later loan" because Lat ū > Alb u. But VrV > ɾ rather than /r/ is also bizarre. Reexamine. Loan possibilitiesː Italian durare, Venetian durar? Unlikelyː Old French durer /dyrer/, Dalmatian duror, Old Occitan durar /dy-/. Vatasescu 1997: 54. Matasovic 2024: 63 
d ˈu r r ɑ t͡ʃ u m , d ˈu r ə s $Durrës. This is a NOVEL proposal though foreshadowed by Bonnet 1998(:37)'sa remarks about the outcome -s- from what looks like a Latin and Greek /kʰiu-/, rather than the expected /c/ <q>. Italian likewise has Durazzo rather than expected Duraccio; Slavic Drač. The idea here is that the Latin Durrachium and perhaps the Greek Dyrrhakhium actually overlie a Proto-Albanian /tʃ/ (using this sound as it is what is used in Proto-Albanian as the reflex of PIE *kʷ before i/e by De Vaan 2018 and Orel 2000), rather than a /kiu/ or /kju/, which is plausible as this sound was lacking in both languages. Regarding the accent, once we no longer have the penultimate -I-, then the /u/ becomes antepenultimate, and the stress on it falls out easily from this proposal! 
ˌeː d ˌi k t ˈɑː n i oː , h ɛ t ˈɔ j $verb: to investigate. Prosthetic h-. Orel 2000: 107. Orel 1998: 146. Alternative is PAlb *skaita?

Cf socio > shoqe etc.) -- even though fallia seems to underlie Italian faglia, French faille etc (Bonnet 1998: 350). Landi 1989: 126; Çabej 1986 (I: 172); Haarmann 1972: 125; Meyer 1891: 98; Mihaescu 1993: 22.

f ˌɑ l k ˈoː n e m , f a j k ˈu a̯ $falcon. Orel 2000: 33, 113. Gheg fajkue. De Vaan 2017: 1742. Haarmann 1972: 45; Mihaescu 1966 : 22. Dialectal forms incl. falkue, falkore , fajkore -- Mann 1948(: 100), Vatasescu 1997(: 213); perhaps more recent (Demiraj 1985: 122 apud Vatasescu). Matasovic 2024: 63.
f ˈɑ k i eː s , f ˈa c ɛ $cheek, page, side, surface. Orel 1998: 93 -- earlier *faq? Singularized plural? While some other sources prefer from facia, Bonnet 1998(:143, 290, 379, cf. esp. 290) strongly asserts inheritance from faciēs. 
f ˌɑ k t ˈuː r ɑ m , f y t ˈy ɾ ə $face. Lack of double rr /r/ in this specific environment is far from unprecedented: cf detyrë, natyrë, shëmbëlltyrë, gjymtyrë, vetyrë, yndyrë. On development, see Orel 2000: 34, 58, 117; also asserted as inherited by Bonnet 1998(: 88, 260, 379). Some however attribute it to a formation based of the native element fyt however. Bonnet 1998(: 120*, 260) asserts vowel harmonization occurred in the initial syllable, and notes Buzuku's "fëtyrë", and (p260) hypothesizes an earlier *fëftyrë.

f ˈɑ r m ɑ k u m , f ˈa ɾ m ə k $venom. Old Albanian, not modern, in Budi. Modern farmak is influenced by Aromanian farmac or Greek. Vatasescu 1997: 480. 
f ˈɑ s k i ɑ m , f ˈa ʃ c ə $bandage, diaper. Modern form fashqe is from singularized plural. Also fashë. Derivative fashaturë. Orel 1998: 94. Bonnet 379. TODO: reconsider actually making fashqe regular...Matasovic 2024: 63.
f ˌɑ s k i ˈo l ɑ m , f c ˈɔ lˠ ə $"flax beaten, combed and prepared for spinnning". Bonnet 1998(:77), Vatasescu 1997: 383. In many dialects, and in the Standard )(?) fjollë. Romanian făș(i)oară / fâșioară is of interest. Orel 1998(: 129) attributes it to a Middle Greek fakiólē (=eta) "handkerchief, napkin". Vatasescu also cites Kristophson 1988(p86) in doubting its Latin provenance as well. On the other hand, Meyer 1891(ː106, apud Orel 1998) separates fjollë from fqollë, attributing fjollë to fileōlum; Cabej 1962(ː32/192) likewise attributes it to fileˈolum without a written long o. This possibility is listed below. On another hand, Vatasescu 1997(ː383-4) provides a *folliol(us/a/um) as the source of fjollë specifically, apparently following Kristophson 1988(ː65) on this and providing Romanian fuior. These alternate possibilties (favoring feminines with tonic short o as the otherwise is the most likely to otherwise work out) are provided below for now. Perhaps reexamine.

f ˈɑː t u m , f ˈa t $luck, chance, fate, spouse. See Schumacher and Matzinger 2013. May be learned loan. Orel 2000(ː30) counts as from ancient Latin. Attributed to early Latin loans because Lat ū > Alb y by Orel 2000: 34. Bonnet 379. Matasovic 2024: 63.
f ˌɑ w ˈɑ l i ɑ m , f ˈɛ l ə $honeycomb. cf Ital fiale. See Orel 2000: 36. Distinct from felë ~ thelë meaning "piece, slice". Alas this is missing in Newmark 1998 and Bonnet 1998...
f ˌe b r u ˈɑː r i u m , f ɾ ˈu a̯ ɾ $February. Only attested as fruer in Gheg -- Orel 2000 : 58. Bonnet 379 as fruerr. De Vaan 2017: 1745 as "Old Gheg fëruor". Bonnet 1998(: 133) notes its attestation as fëruor in Budi, and the dialectal Gheg fruur. Cf. Meyer 1891: 109. Bonnet 1998(:133) defends this as an inheritance from an ancient Latin loan, and uses it as an example elsewhere (Bonnet 1998: 225, 269, 329), with Old Albanian -uor becoming Gheg -uer and Tosk -uar. Later februar is a learned modification of this it seems. Vatasescu 1997: 133 -- dialectal Romanian făurar. Matasovic 2024: 63 as fruer. 
f ˈeː m i n ɑ m , f ˈɛ m ə ɾ $female. Orel 1998: 95. Gheg femën. Bonnet 379 as feminus, but Bonnet 62 and 295 as fēmina. De Vaan 2017: 1735 -- as feːmina.

f ˌeː t ˈoː s u m , f ˈɔ ʃ $Infant. Modern form has now become foshnjë through suffixation. Otherwise may be related to fascia > fashqe. Orel 1998: 102. 
f ˈi d e m , f ˈɛ $religion. Attributed to fide:s by some but fidem by Orel 2000(:29). Orel 2000(:28-9) attributes it to "later layer of Latin loanwords" when short e reappeared. However, the loss of /d/ may be suspicious and suggest a later Romance loan. Candidates include: Old French fei ~ fai ~ foi, Old Occitan fe ~ fei, Venetian fè. Not candidatesː Sicilian fidi, Italian fede. Perhaps via Angevin (cf. Marr 2023). 
f ˌi d ˈɑː n i oː , f ɛ j ˈɔ j $to engage, betroth, ally. Vatasescu 1997 p115 -- despite ohters attributing it to Italian or a derivation off of fe "faith", she attributes this to Latin.

f ˌi s t ˈi k u l ɑ , f ʃ ˈi k u lˠ $whip. Orel 1998: 348. (> verb pshikëlloj "to beat with a cane", fsh > psh by dissimilation?)
f ˌi s t u l ˈɑː n i oː , f i ʃ k ə lˠ ˈɔ j $verb: to whistle. Cf Italian fischiare. Form may be from fisclare instead? See Orel 1998: 97-8. Variantsː pishkëlloj, vishkëlloj. Matasovic 2024: 64.
f l ˈɑ k u l ɑ , f l ˈa k ə $flame. Plural flakëra (Gheg flakëna). Cf Romanian flacără, Italian fiaccola. Orel 1998: 98-9 as a "backformation" of a *flakull, itself from "dialectal" Romance flacula evinced in Romanian flacără, Italian fiaccola, replacing facula "little torch". Note however Campidanese Sardinian flake (Meyer 1891: 107; Bonnet 1998: 271). This seems close to the view of Burr 1975(:63-4). Çabej 1960 argued for a native Albanian formation based on the semantic richness, arguing for an elementary creation on an "expressive base". However, as Bonnet 1998(: 272), these semantic developments are all to parallel to those seen in Romance, especially in Romanian flacără and Istriot fagla. Bonnet 1998(: 272, 379) defends the original etymology of facula here as either a Late Latin or Albanian-internal metathesis, the latter from a *fakël -- though in all of these cases, the plural in -ëra (Gheg -ëna) would have had to be innovated pretty early. Admittedly this is a productive suffix (Orel 2000: 229). Proposal that the plural is actually the inherited form, from a Romance flacula (per Burr 1975, Orel 1998 etc) embedded below. 
f ˈɑ k u l ɑ , f l ˈa k ə $Bonnet 1998(: 272, 379)'s view, see above. TODO remove later once decision on this issue (flakë...) is made.
f l ˈɑ k u l ɑ , f l ˈa k ə ɾ a $flame, plural as inherited forme. See flakë above.
f l ˈɑː m e n , f l ˈa m ə $cold, cholera in animals, epilepsy. Same semantic development as Russian povetrie. Orel 1998: 99. Or should it be from the accusative flāminem? That might cause issues though... 
f l ˈɑ s k ɑ m , f l ˈa ʃ k ə $type of cup. Would have to be a later loan as this military term in Latin is of Germanic origin. TODO consider moving to late Latin loan set if ever established. Vatasescu 1997: 398.

ɡ ˈɑ l ɡ u l u m , ɡ ˈa ɾ ɡ u lˠ $starling, bee-eater (bird). Per Orel 1998: 110, from galbulum "blackbird", with the dialectal garbull being a persistent archaic form. This Latin galbulum seems to have referred to a nut though (TODO: cite this), while the galgulum meant witwall, a bird. Bonnet 1998(:252, 275) attributes this to galgulum. Vatasescu 1997 (:213) notes the variant gargëll cited in Mann 1948(: 122), as well as Daco-Romanian grangur and dialectal gungur, and Aromanian gangur (an adjective meaning "verde închis, verde gălbui". 
ɡ ˌɑ l l i ˈɑː t i ɑ , ɡ l ˈa s ə $bird dropping(s). Actually gëlasë? Romanian găinaț < gallīnātia per Orel (1998: 118), though for Meyer 1891 it is from < gallīnācea, which is attested (cf. Friulian gjalinace, It gallinaccio, Romanian găinaț, Occ galinat, Span gallinaz-o, -a). (why not gallātia?) Orel 2000: 118. French/Angevin are implausible here but some Romance loan may be at work. Vatasescu 1997(: 366) has it as gëlasë, consider restoring that.

ɡ ˈe n t e m , ɟ ˈi n d $folk, people. Per Orel 1998(: 136), plural only; masculine, as gjinde, may instead be from pl. gen. gentium -- or from some Italian variety later. Orel 2000(:27) argues it is a "later Latin" loan, but from Latin. However De Vaan 2017(:1743) and Bonnet 1998(:57, 143, 380) use gjind unproblematically from gentem, and gjind is also the citation form in Newmark 1998(: 285), not gjinde. Bonnet 1998(: 116) does observe regional differences however: gjëndëje in Southern Albania, gjind skewing toward Gheg (and lacking a nasal vowel), and gjende in Greece. Bonnet 1998(: 292) notes a "gjint". Matasovic 2024: 64 as gjinde.

ɡ l ˈɑ n d e m , l ˈə n d ə $acorn, gland (equated to French gland which means both by Bonnet 1998 -- "acorn" is the only meaning supported in Newmark 1998(:452). Bonnet 1998(: 249) -- lëndë is Tosk; CHam and some Tosk have lënde; Gheg has lênn, lend, and land. Meyer 1891(:243) notes the plural lëndre in CHam/Arv in Greece, and Romanian ghindă, and Aromanian and Megl. glîndã. Çabej 1960 prefers a native etymology, connected to lënde "lens, lentil", which Bonnet 1998(:250) sees as more likely from Latin lentem itself. However, this latter lënde "lens, lentil" is not in Newmark 1998(; 452), which lists lënde as "matter, timber, acorn". It appears that form in -a was inherited in Balkan Romance, and much of the rest of Romance --REW p281-2 (n.3778). 
ɡ l ˈɑ n d u l ɑ m , ɟ ˈə n d ə ɾ $gland. Gheg: gjandra. Cf Romanian ghindură, Aromanian gljindură. Sometimes listed as gjendër with /ɛ/. Orel 1998: 135 as gjëndër. Bonnet 1998: 49, 241, 380 also as gjëndër. De Vaan 2017: 1742 as gjëndër. Gheg form: gjândër for Bonnet 1998(ː46), gjandër for Orel 1998(ː135). Orel 1998(:135) notes Tosk variants glëndër and grëndël "reflect the anlaut gl- and thus exclude the Italian etymology". Bonnet (p246, 250) notes also glëndërë (Arvanitika), grëndëlë (some other Arvanitika and in Cham), gianerr (Northwest Gheg) and gandër (Dibran). May be Angevin -- cf. Marr 2023. Vatasescu 1997(ː28) however- Arvanitika glëndër.
ɡ l ˈe m u m , l ˈə m $clew, yarn. Rom. ghem. Vatasescu 1997 p84. 
ɡ l ˈiː r e m , ɟ ˈɛ ɾ $dormouse. May be native instead.  Attributed to "another stage of contacts" in which Lat ī > Alb e per Orel 2000: 34. Bonnet 1998: 380. Arbëreshë has lir and ler; these may be later Romance loans (cf. Neapolitan gliero, gliro; Fr. loir, etc.) or a separate etym/item (see below). Some Gheg has ger, and some dialects have ger instead as meaning "squirrel" (Bonnet 1998: 247). See below.

ɡ r ˈe ɡ e m , ɡ ɾ ˈi ɟ $herd. Alt form grigje, Orel cites grigj as inherited .Orel 2000(:27) argues it is a "later Latin" loan, but from Latin. Bonnet 380. De Vaan 2017: 1734. 
ɡ r ˈe m i u m , ɡ ɾ ˈɛ m b $boson. Bonnet 161. 
ɡ r ˈi p s ɑ , ɡ r ˈi f ʃ ə $jay, shrewish woman, magpie, strawberry tree. May instead be related to "krip" hair. But connected to Latin and Romance gripsa by Orel 2000(ː119). 
ɡ r ˌu ˈi l l ɑ m , k ɔ j ɾ ˈi l ə $crane. Bonnet 1998(:134) -- a difficult case, for which he proposes a complicated sequence of events involving repeated metathesis. Variants: gërrilë, kurrilë, kurrillë, kojrillë.  Vatasescu 1997(:213) lists kurrilë as primary 
ɡ r ˌuː m u l ˈɑː n i o , ɡ ɾ u m b u lˠ ˈɔ j $verb: to gather. Vatasescu 1997: 314. 
ɡ r ˈuː m u l u s , ɡ ɾ ˈu m b u lˠ $heap. Bonnet 1998: 88. Vatasescu 1997: 226.  
ɡ ˈu l ɑ m , ɡ ˈɔ j ə $mouth. In Arbereshe and Arvanitika -- golë. Vatasescu 1997: 25, Mann 1948: 129. Cf. Romanian gura. Tagliavini 1937: 123 and Euler 1985(:117) apud Vatasescu argue for an Italian loan, which may be plausible. Vatasescu cites Huld 1984(:66) to state that -ll- would be expected internally in Albanian (from Latin). 
ɡ ˈu m m ɑ , ɡ ˈɔ m ə $resin. Per Orel 2000: 29. "same chronological layer" as forms with e as reflex for Latin stressed short i. 
ɡ ˈu n n ɑ m , ɡ ˈu n ə $thick hooded cape of goal wool. Vatasescu 1997: 80 -- Cabej 1962 rejects, later accepts (1970). 
ɡ ˈu t t ɑ m , ɡ ˈu t ə $gout. Orel 2000: 118. Old French gout probably not necessary here.
ɡ ˈyː r u m , ɟ ˈi ɾ $swirling; circular motion of water or wind. Old Alb gjir & gjër. Variant ngjir. = Romanian jur, Old Romanian giur; imprejur < in per giurum (Mihaescu 1966ː 63 apud Vatasescu). Vatasescu 1997: 272. 
h ˈa e̯ d u m , ˈɛ ð $buck. Vatasescu 1997: 201, Meyer 1891: 98. Pellegrini 1980(: 68) raises possibility of a slightly later Latin loan on this; Cabej 1960: 105-6 is skeptical as is Huld 1984: 61. Cf. Romanian ied "buck", and also Aromanian ed, Sardinian edu, Latin haedus, even if rare in Romance, is a regularly corresponding IE cognate to Germanic *gaits (Old English gāt, Old Saxon gēt, Old High German geiz, Old Norse geit, Gothic gaits). 
h ˈɑ s t ɑ m , h ˈɛ ʃ t ə $spear. A rival view asserts this based on hell + -shtë (Jokl 1923: 214, 330; Çabej 1986 I: 238; Orël 1998: 146). However the anc. Bonnet 1998(: 230-1) strongly disputes this account and upholds the Latin etymology, originally of Meyer 1891(:151). TODO reexamine, I must remark I find neither etymology confusing (the Latin itself of uncertain origin and common origin between Alb heshtë and Lat hasta in a third language can't quite be excluded...). 
h ˌɑ s t ˈiː l i ɑ , ʃ t ˈi j ə $spear. Per De Vaan 2017: 1740 and Bonnet 1999: 230, 388. Also supported by Orel. May be related to Alb shtie though... TODO Reexamination may be warranted. 
h ˌi b e r n ˈiː n u m , v ə r ˈi $winter pasture. Gheg: vërrî. Orel 2000: 112. Orel 1998: 505-6. Bonnet 380. Meyer 1891(:471), Mihaescu 1966(:31) apud Vatasescu 1997(:360), alas Vatasescu also notes the rival etymology in hibernīvum, cf. Aromanian arniu "wintering, hibernating", citing Cabej, Papahagi and Poghirc. Bonnet 1998(:129-30, 212, 230) meanwhile suspects a secondary suffixation in -i, but does support hibernus as the root and argues that this does indicate "that the spirantisation (of /b/) is quite ancient" (Bonnet 1998: 167). Regarding Meyer 1891's hypothesis in hibernīnum, Bonnet 1998(:335) regards it unnecessary, but he does provide evidence that in Gheg there is evidence of nasality in placenames: Vrrîth, in the Albanian Alps (though in Pukë, the other placename with this root, Vrrizë, appears to orthographically be without nasality); he explains this nasality as a later and secondary Gheg nasalization. 
h ˈi n n i k u l ɑ , h ˈi ŋ ɡ ə l $girth. Orel 1998: 148. Reexamine.
h ˌi n n i k u l ˈɑː n i oː , h i ŋ ɡ ə lˠ ˈɔ j $verb: to neigh, bleat, yell, from *hinnicula:re, from hinnire. Orel 1998: 148; Çabej 1986(VII: 221). 
h ˈe b d o m ɑ s , j a v ə $week. May need to remove, very bizarre. Orel 2000: 25. 
h ˌe r n i z ˈɑː n i oː , ɾ ə n d͡z ˈɔ j $verb: to cause a hernia. Orel 2000: 103. Orel 1998: 370. 
h o n ˈoː r e m , n d ˈɛ ɾ $honor, fame, reputation. Orel 1998: 283-4. Vatasescu 1997: 55. 
h ˈoː r ɑ m , h ˈɛ ɾ ə $time, instance. Orel 2000(ː32)-- among "the oldest Latin loanwords". Orel 1998: 146. De Vaan 2017: 1735. Orë, the "element geg căruia îi corespunde tosc fati.." seems rather difficult, but cf Mihaescu 1981 219 (apud Vatasescu 1997: 481).
h ˌo r r ˈeː n i oː , u r ˈɛ j $verb: to hate. Same semantic shift in Romanian urî "to hate". Variant: urroj. Orel 2000: 107. Orel 1998: 490. Vatasescu 1997: 55; Çabej 1967 (:88-9) attributes the Romanian to the Albanian. 
h ˌo s p ˈi t i u m , ʃ t ə p ˈi $house. Has issues. But most sources attribute this to Latin. Orel 1998: 438. De Vaan 2017: 1737
i ˈɑː n u ɑ , ɟ ˈu a̯ $bee-hive. Now zgjua from prefix insertion, zgjue in Gheg. Orel 2000: 54. Orel 1998: 524. 
i ˌɑː n u ˈɑː r i u m , j a n ˈa ɾ $January. Asserted to be a "later" later Latin loan by Orel 1998(:xix). Bonnet 1998(: 167, 234, 334) proposes instead it came to Albanian via Greek ɣenaris, though this still doesn't explain the vowel quality as you would expect a > e in the stressed penultimate due to i-mutation, and the countertonic antepenultimate /a/ is also unexpected. May need to reexamine.
i ˌe i uː n ˈɑː n i oː , ɟ ə ɾ ˈɔ j $verb: to try, taste. Orel 1998. Now has become ngjëroj in most dialects. Gheg: gjinonj, ngjinonj. Orel 2000: 54.

ˈiː l i k e m , ˈi l c ɛ $holly/holm oak. Orel 1998(: 154) has it as ilqë with ilqe as a singularized plural.  Orel 1998: 154. Me: perhaps i:likem > i:lkiem earlier in this case?

ˌi m p e r ˈɑː t u m , m b ɾ ˈɛ t $king. Variant: mpret. Gheg: mret. Arvaniteː mbret. Orel 2000: 114 -- may be imparātus (he who defends). Or imperātus (he who rules) -- Orel 1998:252.  De Vaan 2017: 1743 as from imperātor, likewise Bonnet 1998(: 38, 44, 207*. Bonnet 1998(: 38) argues it can't come from the accusative imparātōrem and thus prefers the vocative-nominative; for the loss of -(ë)r, he asserts there was a truncation, as with praebyter > prift, with the -r- surviving in Tosk mbretëreshë and mbretëria (the -n- in Gheg for these, he asserts, is analogical). Line added below for that hypothesis. Old Albanian had mpëret -- Kuvendi in Arbënit (Vatasescu 1997: 426). On phrasiological equivalences between Romanian and Albanian for this word family, see Vatasescu 1997: 425-6. Matasovic 2024: 64. 
ˌi m p e r ˈɑː t o r , m b ɾ ˈɛ t ə ɾ $king. From the nominative per Bonnet. See above. 
ˌi m p l ˌɑ n t ˈɑː n i oː , m b ə l t ˈɔ j $verb: to plant. Orel 1998: 250. 
ˌi m p l ˈeː n i oː , m b l ˈɔ j $verb: to fill. Attributed by Orel to "the earliest stages of Albanian contacts with Romance", the "oldest layer demonstrating ... Lat ē > Alb o, similar to the change of EPA *ē"... reexamine. Orel 1998: 252. Matasovic 2024: 64.
ˌi m m ˈuː n e , m ˈu $just right, exactly. Gheg mû. (Why not my?) Orel 1998: 276.

ˌi n d ˌi r ˈeː k t u m , n d ɾ ˈɛ c $verb: to put in order, arrange. From dialectal (n)dreq (who said this?). May be partially or completely from Aromanian (cf Aro ndreg, Ro drege) -- may need to remove. Cf Orel 1998: 287, who asserts a derivation from a "ndreq ~ ndrejt". Bonnet 1998(:348)-- "l'association d'un verbe "fabriquer" à l'adjectif "droit" est si prégnante que la variante phonétique dreq/dëreq de drejt aussi a été tiré un verbe albanaisː ndreq "arranger, ajuster". Alternative hypothesis below. 
ˌi n d ˌi r ˈeː k t i oː , n d ɾ ˈɛ c $verb: to put in order, arrange. Hypothesis noted by Vatasescu 1998: 308-9. In Buzuku -- ëndëreq. Arbereseh nd(ë)reqem in passive form. Çabej 1964(t4 :85 apud Vatasescu) quite reasonably notes some phonological issues here that may need explanation. Romanian a îndrepta. (note also the directicāre attached to Romanian a deretica, which is also semantically close.). 
ˌi n d u l ɡ ˈeː n i oː , n d j ˈɛ j $verb: to forgive compassionately. Old Albanian: ndëljenj. Bonnet 380 as ndëjej. Orel 1998(ː286) as ndjej. Çabej 1960: apud Bonnet 1998(:253-4); Bonnet unlike Çabej finds this word troublesome in the lg > j development, explaining it in either phonotactic repair or influence from Byzantine Greek (ex-)omologew but it's not quite clear why this Byz word in particular. Vatasescu 1997(:426) notes Old Albanian ndëliej and ndëlenj, and the derived femine noun ndëlesë in Buzuku. This Latin etymon is among the cases where Latin etyma survive in Albanian but not in Romanian (Vatasescu 1997: 496).  Root verbnot attested.

ˌi n s ˈi ɡ n i ɑ m , ʃ ˈɛ ɲ ə $sign, symbol. Bonnet 1998: 187-8, 225. Coalesced with reflex of SIGNVM and synchronically treated as a single lexeme (Newmark 1998: 798-9), but the etymons may be best treated separately -- cf Bonnet 1998(:187-8), Mihaescu 1993(:42,46). 
ˌiː n s ˈu b u l u m , ʃ ˈu l $beam, bar, roller -- in Newmark 1998(: 848) "wooden pole; gate bar, door bolt; gun bolt; roller bar... tie beam with roof truss". Vatasescu (1997: 386) is unsertain and argues *shull is expected, noting that Cabej 1962 argued for a native etymology; alas Mihaescu 1993(:130) and others cite it, and Bonnet 1998(: 85, 225) uses it as a basis for regular diachronic phonology (of Latin short /u/, and /ns/, in Albanian).

k ˌɑ l ɑ m ˈeː t̪ u m , k a lˠ m ˈɛ t $Kallmet (placename). In Zadrima, Lezhe district. Bonnet 323/376. 
k ˈɑ l ɑ m u m , k ˈa lˠ m $reed, cattail, pipe. May be native. Variant form: kallëm. Orel 1998: 167. Bonnet 337 as kallëm. 
k ˌɑ l ˈɑ n d a e̯ , k lˠ ˈa n ə $New Year's Eve. Orel 1998: 186. Bonnet 376 as kullana. Orel lists variants: kullana, kollane, kellane, kllande. Vatasescu as këllana, kullana "first snow, new year's eve". Cabej 1962 p174.
k ˌɑ l e n d ˈɑː r i u m , k a lˠ ə n d ˈu a̯ ɾ $January. Seems to largely be a Gheg form but non-analogized Tosk would-be form used. Topalli 2017: 703. Variants: kallënduar (TODO check if this is in Topalli -- it's not in Bonnet), kallënduor; kallnor (per Topalli 2017: 703). Bonnet 376 as kallnuar (329: Gheg kallnuer). 
k ˈɑ l i k e m , c ˈɛ l c $glass (for drinking, material too). Orel 2000: 24, 57. Bonnet (376) lists as calix, but as inherited from calicem (44/252). More indepth discussion of diachronyː see Bonnet 45, 204, 252. De Vaan 2017: 1735.
k ˌɑ l k ˈɑː r i ɑ m , k ə l c ˈɛ ɾ ɛ $limestone. Now gëlqere, but earlier këlqere, says Jozef in Topalli 2017. Orel (2000: 30) treats këlqere as inherited. Also Orel 1998: 176. Bonnet 376 lists as gëlqere, but treats këlqerë as inherited too (p252/332; 332 on suffix). 
k ˈɑ l l u m , c ˈɛ l $scab. Orel 2000: 58. From plural calli of a masculine version callus??? Orel 1998: 355. 
k ˈɑ l tʰ ɑ , k ˈa l t ə $light blue. Now mostly kaltër; Bonnet 293/376 treats this as regular on its own. 
k ˈɑ l tʰ i n u m , k ˈa l t ə ɾ $light blue. Older form: kaltënë. Orel 2000: 57. 
k ˈɑ m b ɑ , k ˈə m b ə $leg, foot. Gheg kâmbë. Orel 1998: 177 cites both *camba and *gamba but notes *camba has "no traces ... in Balkan Romance". Huld is unsure of the direction of borrowing. Mann, Camarda, Meyer-Lübke, and Tagliavini see it as native. Mihaescu, Corominas, Orel earlier and Robles see it as borrowed from Greek. C̟abej and Huld are unclear on direction of borrowing. We follow Bonnet (376/48/118  who asserts a derivation from *camba with confidence. De Vaan 2017: 1736 also supports "camba".
k ˌɑ m b i ˈɑː n i oː , k ə m b ˈɛ j $verb: to exchange, reciprocate. Bonnet 161 holds the form from Latin to be cambiāre as does Vatasescu (p71); Schumacher holds(2013ː 33) holds cambīre. 
k ˈɑ m e r ɑ , c ˈɛ m ə ɾ $snake's coil. Orel 1998: 356. 
k ˌɑ m p ˈɑː n ɑ , k ə m b ˈa ɾ ə $bell. Only attested as Gheg këmbanë, Tosk is a loan from Gheg. Variants: kummona in Gheg. 
k ˌɑ m p ˈo n ɑ , k ə m b ˈɔ ɾ ə $bell. Doublet of the above. Orel 1998: 177; Orel 2000: 114. Jokl attributes to Dalmatian campuone (Orel 1998: 177). Gheg: këmbonë (+kummona?)
k ˌɑ m ˈi s i ɑ m , k ə m ˈi ʃ ə $shirt. Orel 1998: 177.
k ˈɑ n ɑ p e m , k ˈə ɾ p $hemp. Gheg variant kanp. Orel 2000: 56. Matasovic 2024(:62) -- Gheg kânëp. Vatasescu 1997 p179 noting Old Tosk kërëp, Tosk kërp, alongside Old Gheg kanëp and modern Gheg kânp. 
k ˌɑ n d ˈeː l ɑ m , k ə n d ˈɛ lˠ ə $lamp. Orel 2000 p25. Orel 1998: 178. Bonnet 376. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
k ˈɑ n e m , c ˈɛ n $dog. Old Alb kjen? Gjakova Gheg: qan. Lack of Tosk rhotacism may be suspicious, opening the door that this may be a later loan. Candidates that marry phonological and historical plausibility include Old French chien cjɛn. Norman quien (cf Picard kien) is not an option here, nor is Occitan can. Seems a very likely Angevin loan. Note the vowel is not an issue for native though -- just the n rather than /ɾ/, which can in fact be explained as a Gheg to Tosk loan. Alternative from Latin after all ː < CANINVS -- I think Eric Hamp wrote on that somewhere (find that). Orel 1998(:356) instead argues it's a "late loanword.. or rather from [Latin]'s Balkan continuations (Rum câine etc), reflecting no traces of rhotacism in Tosk... Camarda argues it is native from a PIE *cwōn, as does Hamp. De Vaan 2017: 1735 -- supports canem. 
k ˈɑ n n ɑ , k ˈa n ə $reed. Orel 1998: 168. Vatasescu 1997(:180).
k ˌɑ n n ˈɑː t ɑ m , k ə n ˈa t ə $pitcher, clay vessel for liquids. Bulgarian /kənatə/ as well, but word is generally more characteristic of Western than Balkan Romance. Cf Cabej 1962(p188), Vatasecu 1997 p 396. Kristophson apud Vatasescu attributes it to Italian. 
k ˌɑ n n ˈeː t u m , k ə n ˈɛ t $swamp, marsh, valley. Orel 1998: 178.
k ˌɑ n t ˈɑː n i oː , k ə n d ˈɔ j $verb: to sing. Orel 1998: 178. Bonnet 376. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
k ˈɑ n tʰ ɑ r i s , k ˈa n d ə r $insect. Orel 2000: 58.  
k ˈɑ n tʰ ɑ r u s , k ˈa n d ə ɾ $jar, vessel. Orel 1998: 168. Alternatively, per Çabej St I 264, from Greek kántharos "drinking come"
k ˈɑ n t i k ɑ m , k ˈə n ɡ ə $song. Earlier form: kantkë, këntkë; also këndgë (Bonnet 172*). Orel posits it's from cantic-um with -u-. Orel 2000:116; Orel 1998: 179. Gheg: kangë or kângë. Listed as from canticum in Bonnet 376/49/115/172*, also in De Vaan 2017(ː1736). Form canticam, with -a- from Topalli 2017: 1409 -- going with Topalli here. 
k ˌɑ n t i k ˈe l l u m , k ə ɲ ɟ ˈɛ l $song. Orel 1998: 168; Bonnet 1999ː 321. Listed as kangjél in Orel but këngël in Bonnet. Cabej argues for internal derivation from këngë + el but +el suffixes aren't that common (as opposed to -ull and -ill), and Romanian căntecel suggests Latin canticellum. Vatasescu labels it as Arvanite and Arberehse, listing kangjel, kangele, kangejell.. also Romanian cântecel.
k ˌɑ p p ˈe l l ɑ , k a p ˈɛ l ə $hat. (<cappellus but feminized?). May instead be from Italian cappello. Topalli 2017: 713.
k ˈɑ p r ɑ , c ˈɛ p ə ɾ $f, pl qepra. "Beam, roof-beam". Jokl 1923 attributes to a Romance capreus. Orel 1998: 357. Jokl 1923 p268 apud Vatasescu 1997 p97 argues for CAPREUS here. 
k ˌɑ p r ˈe o l u m , k a p ɾ ˈu a̯ lˠ $roe deer. Variant: kapruell in Gheg, kapruall in Tosk; also kaproll. Orel 1998: 170. Bonnet 376. Bonnet 35/75/344 on kapruall/kaprolli. Lack of yod -- Bonnet 132. On first syll -- Bonnet 209. Old Alb kapruoll in Buzuku -- Vatasescu p206. +Matasovic 2024: 61.
k ˈɑ p r e u s , c ˈɛ p ə ɾ $ hoe ("binette" pour Bonnet). Bonnet 44/376. 
k ˌɑ p i s s ˈo n i oː , k a f ʃ ˈɔ j $verb: to bite. May instead be derivation off of kafshë "beast". Old Tosk najzonj, dialectal ajsonj, putative Old Albanian *knavshonj.... very bizarre. captiāre per Meyer -- cf. Vatasescu 1997 (p 41) for discussion. +Matasovic 2024: 61 as capess-o. 
k ˌɑː p ˈoː n e m , k a p ˈu a̯ $rooster. Gheg kapue. Mpl kaponj. Orel 1998: 170. Bonnet 376 as ca:po: (but Bonnet always lists nominative it seems). Orel 2000: 33. Old Gheg (Bardhi) and modern Gheg kapue vs. Tosk kapua (pl. kaponj) -- Mann 1948(:184), Vatasescu 1998(:211). 
k ˈɑ p t iː , c ˈɛ f t $drinking glass. Orel 1998: 354. 
k ˌɑ p t i ˈɑː t ɑ m , k a f ʃ ˈa t ə $bite (noun). May instead just be derived more recently from kafshoj but seems phonologically plausible that it came from the supine or the participle. TODO: get citation for this, not in Orel 1998 or Bonnet 1998.

k ˈɑ r r ɑ , k ˈa r ə $cart. Latin carrum "two wheeled wagon" (apparently feminized?). Orel 2000: 59.

k ˌɑ r s ˈɑː n i ɑ m , ɡ ə ɾ ʃ ˈə ɾ ə $shears. Orel 1998: 116. Plural is gërshëra. May be native of form ger+shere, cf gërthapë, gërshet, gëthep, gërreç. But Gheg has gërshanë. k ˌɑ s t ɑ n ˈeː t u m , k a ʃ ɲ ˈɛ t $chestnut grove. Orel 2000(:32) -- "presumably borrowed after the shift ē > EPA ā had taken place but before the diphthongization of short EPA *e, i.e. at the time when EPA *e was the best possible substitute for Lat ē". Orel 1998: 173. Bonnet 376/323.
k ˌɑ s t ˈe l l ɑ m , k ə ʃ t j ˈɛ lˠ ə $castle, fort. Orel 1998: 185; Çabej 1986 (I: 281-2). 
k ˌɑ s t ˈe l l u m , k ə ʃ t j ˈɛ l $citadel -- now archaic. In Buzuku. Orel 1998: 185. 
k ˈɑ s u l ɑ , k ˈa t͡ʃ u l ə $hood. Now kaçule, not kaçulë becuase it's a singularized plural. Earlier kashulë -- Orel 2000(ː103) thinks sh > ç as in keçile and balçëm. Variant kaçulle. Cf Romanian căciulă. Given stress pattern and that we have /l/ not /lˠ/, perhaps was actually from casúllam?.  Albanian kaçule > Romanian căciulă > Alb k̈ësulë per Orel 1998(:184). 
k ˈɑ w k ɑ m , k ˈa f k ə $skull. Vatasescu 1997: 396-7 but see discussion (rejected by Kristophshon, may be Byzantine instead).
k ˈɑ w s ɑ m , k ˈa f ʃ ə $beast. Attributed to a stratum of later loanwords after EPA *au had already gone to *a by Orel 2000(:35), likewise by Bonnet (p107). Alternatively attributed to Dalmatian or something else Romance (Occitan causa /kawzo/? Romanian cauză?) by Bonnet 1998(:109). Relatively Late Balkan Romance -- per Orel 1998: 163. Bonnet 377. Bonnet 106 -- alt etymology to CAPSA. Bonnet 107 -- "late loan". Bonnet 109 -- Dalmatian loan is unnec. hypothesis. 
k ˈɑ w u l ɑ m , ɡ ˈa v ə ɾ $hole. Romanian gaură, Aromanian gavrã. Loan from Balkan Romance? Vatasescu 1997: 237. Or from a *cavanum? TODO reconsider. 
k ˈe l l ɑ , c ˈɛ l ə $priest's house. Orel 1998: 355. Bonnet 377/54/143. Qelza (bonnet 377) seems to be a derivation not an independent etymon.
k ˌe l l ˈɑː n i oː , c ə lˠ ˈɔ j $verb: to beat, hit, gain, obtain. Orel 1998: 360. May be via Lat percellāre > unatt *për-qëlloj. Meyer asserts from a Romance cellāre as we do here. 
k ˈe n t u m , c ˈi n d $hundred. Orel 1998: 361. Bonnet 377/57/143. Cabej 1960 thinks it is a singularized plural (Bonnet 198-9). De Vaan 2017: 1734. +Matasovic 2024: 61.

k ˈeː p ɑ , c ˈɛ p ə $onion (mass noun). Cf Romanian ceapă. Gheg: kepë. Orel 1998(ː357). North Toskː qep, not to be confused with qep < PAlb *klepa. Bonnet 377. Vatasescu 175.
k ˌeː p ˈu l l ɑ , c ɛ p ˈu l ɛ $bulb of a plant. Orel 1998(:357). Pl also qepule. Inherited singular qepulë, now singularized plural? Reexamine.
k ˌe r ɑ s i ˈɑː r i u m , c ɛ ɾ ʃ ˈu a̯ ɾ $June. Listed as qershuar in Bonnet 1999(ː329,377), but much more common as qershor in modern Albanian.  Cf Romanian cireșar < cerasiārius. May instead be derived from qershi+ uar(>or). "Monophthongization" of uar to ɔr implied per Bonnet 1998:329. 
k ˌe r e b ˈe l l u m , k ɾ ˈɛ l $curl. Orel 2000: 25. TODO reexamine, possible IE root -- cf English curl. Note also widespread sound symbolism in kr- for curled objects.. 
k ˌe r ˈe b r u m , k ɾ ˈy e̯ $head. Vatasescu 1997: 22, Meyer 1891: 206 (or cerebellum, but prefers cerebrum). A difficult case, may instead be native alas Demiraj 1985 apud Vatasescu "evidențiază și dificultățile fonetice ale etimologie autothtone). Cf also Tagliavini 1935-6: 24. 
k ˌe r ˈe s i ɑ , c ɛ ɾ ʃ ˈi $cherry. Meyer 1891: 225, but many others, since Camarda and including Bonnet 1998(: 334) and Orel 1998(: 358) attribute it instead to Greek, which does seem more likely given the accentuation. Cf Romanian cireașă (-> Blg cheresha), Aromanian cireashă, cireashi, Basque gerezi, West Gmc *kirsija, Celtic *keresya, Ligurian çêxa etc... TODO probably remove and place in Exclusions file. 
k ˈe r r u s , c ˈa r $bitter oak. Orel 2000: 25. De Vaan 2017: 1735.
k ˌe r r ˈeː t u m , c ɛ r ˈɛ t $toponym, Qerret. In Kavaje region. Bonnet 1999: 323. 
kʰ ˌe r s ˈy d r ɑ , k u l ʃ ˈɛ d ə ɾ $Kulshedra (Albanian hydra equivalent). Alternate form: kuçedër. Both have plural and definite -ra. Feminine. Attributed by Mihăescu, Orel (1998: 201), Bonnet 1999(:148, 274, 377) etc to Lat chersydrus. ̠-Ra as feminine. Camarda argues it is native in coinage: kulish / kuç + hydra -- this may be plausible. Refer to Topalli and Cabej if possible to reexamine. 
k ˌe r t ˈɑː n i oː , c ə ɾ t ˈɔ j $verb: to quarrel. Orel 1998: 354 as qerta (?). Bonnet 377 as qërtoj "to reprimanɗ". Romanian a certa. Vatasescu 1998(: 52) as qërtoj 
k i ˈeː n i oː , c ˈɔ j $verb: to wake up. Attributed by Orel to "the earliest stages of Albanian contacts with Romance", the "oldest layer demonstrating ... Lat ē > Alb o, similar to the change of EPA *ē"... reexamine, note also zgjoj (is this really not the same etymon??). Orel 1998: 362. Lat had forms with infs ciēre and cīre. 
k ˌiː k ˈɑː l ɑ , ɟ i n k ˈa lˠ ə $cicada. Bonnet 377. Vatasescu 1997: 220 as to qingallë, cf also Janson 1986(ː 120). Rom. cicoare. 
k ˈi k e r e m , c ˈi c ə ɾ $chickpea. Orel 1998: 361-2 as from cicerem. (qiqiriq -- derivative) Bonnet 377/67/143 (as cicer), 66(as from cicerum).  
k ˌi kʰ ˈo r eː ɑ , k ˈɔ ɾ ɛ $chicory. Orel 1998 (ː191) attributes it to Latin cichorēum but the root in Italian and etc has antepenultimate stress. Bonnet 377 (as cichōrēum).   Romanian cicoare. Vatasescu 1997:180. 
k ˌi k ˈuː t ɑ , k u k ˈu t ə $fennel, hemlock. A "later Latin loan" per Orel 2000(:34) -- reexamine for possibility it is from Romance insteadː cf Romanian and Aromanian cucută. 
k ˈiː m i k e m , c ˈi m k ə $bug. Listed as "çimkë" in Orel 2000(ː34) -- probably that's Gheg. Qimqë also attested. Qimkë is regular per Bonnet 146. 
k ˌi m ˈu s s ɑ m , c i m ˈu ʃ ə $non-woven edge of a canvas. Latin cimussa was a rare word with an unknown etymology in Latin and reflected only in Italo-Romance it seems at first glance. This etymology is proposed by Çabej (see Çabej 1982: 105, 248), but the Latin word could in fact be derived from Proto-Albanian in this case, or both from something else obscure.
k ˈi n ɡ u l ɑ , q ˈi ŋ ɡ ə l $saddle belt , girth, dwarf elder (plant), danewort. Def sg: qingla. Latin >> *cingila? Orel 2000(:361) -- fpl. Bonnet 377/65-67/186-188. More common form is qingël but qengël is "plus regulier" per Bonnet 1999(ː67). Vatasescu 1997(:171) notes variants kingël, kingjël, qingjël, qingjëlë. 
k ˌi r k ˈɑː n i oː , k ə ɾ k ˈɔ j $verb: to search. Orel 1998: 181 -- argues was qërkoj but became kërkoj through assimilation of velars. But this is unnattested. Bonnet 377/126/148/297. De Vaan 2017: 1743. 
k ˌi r k ˈe l l ɑ , k ə ɾ c ˈɛ l ə $large knife worn on belt. Per Weigand, via Orel 1998(:182).
k ˌi r k ˈe l l u s , c ˈɛ ɾ θ u lˠ $circuit, wrap reel, yarnwindle, shoots growing out of plant stalk etc. Attributed to a P-Alb *kertsula from Latin circellus. But the timing seems odd here. Alternatively inherited from PIE *ker-c- "to turn, bind". May require further inspection. Cf Demiraj 1997ː 340-341. 
k ˈi r k u l u s , c ˈa ɾ k u lˠ $circle. Orel 1998: 354. Camarda (p138) and Çabej 1986(II: 57) instead posit a Greek loan. Others have hte same etymology as Orel, which goes back to Mezer 1891(:220). 
k ˈi r k u s , c ˈa ɾ k $circle. Orel 2000: 29 -- a "relatively early borrowing". 
k ˈi t r u m , c ˈi t ə ɾ $citron. Orel 1998: 362.
k ˌiː w i t ˈɑː t e m , c y t ˈɛ t $city. Orel 2000: 36. Orel 1998: 364. Bonnet 377. De Vaan 2017: 1735. Bonnet 1998: 46 -- notes that Hahn mentions some regional forms kiudat and qudat... odd.

k l ˌɑː r ˈɑː n i oː , c ə ɾ ˈɔ j $verb: to clean (wheat of weeds.); to cut (vine), a clean (trees); to peel a fruit; to brighten. Old Albanian (Gheg) me qëruem in Bardhi and Bogdani. Derived sqaroj. This is not the only possible explanation however. Vatasescu 1997: 253. Cf also Kristophson 1988: 63. TODO reexamine?

k l ˌɑ w s ˈuː r ɑ , k ə l t͡s ˈy ɾ ə $toponym, Këlcyrë, a city in southern Albania near Përmet. Unclear if this or këshyrë is the regular result. In Byzantine Greek the city was Kleisoura (per Bonnet 246, Kleisoura is itself a Greek blend of kleíw and Latin clausūra). May be via Byzantine Greek? May need to reexamine. Bonnet 1998(:246) does suggest the sibilant's outcome also suggests a more recent entry into Albanian.

Cabej 1960 finds it to be regular however. Reexamine with Topalli?  As clausūra in De Vaan 2017: 1736. +Matasovic 2024: 62.
k l ˌeː m ˈeː n t e m , k ɛ l m ˈɛ n d $Kelmend, toponym (north of Malesia) and name. Bonnet 377. 
k l ˌo m ˈo s t r u m , c ˈu m ə ʃ t $milk. Cham, Arb, Arv : klumësht. Orel 2000: 55, 113. Orel 1998: 362. Orel uses qymësht as seems to be the outcome in some varieties, but I"m going here with qumësht, which was taught to me as standard and used in other sources. Vatasescu 1997(:365) uses qumesht. Bonnet 1998(:85) notes, using qumësht, notes Buzuku's form klomësht -- in the same sentence he notes buzku's lëkondunë for lëkundur so the -on- for Buzuku may be ... [ʊn]?

C'est toujours possible, mais il est finalement aussi simple de le ramener à coopertūra... analogique de apertūra emprunté directement, ou moins probablement par l'intermédiare de dalm. kerpatur, conservé par sbcr krpatur "rembourage". WIll be little evidence though. krat has no entry on fjalorthi -- nor do kret, gratë, kratë, kretë. Orel 1998 cites Meyer 1891

k ˌu k ˈu r b i t ɑ , k ˈu l t ə $watermelon, gourd. Orel 2000: 103; Orel 1998: 202-3. Form now kulte, presumably a singularized plural? l ˌɑ b oː r ˈɑː n i oː , l ə ɾ ˈɔ j $verb: to plough, till, cultivate. Orel 1998: 205. Vatasescu 1998(: 326) -- there is also a variant lëvroj. 
l ˌɑː b r ˈu s k ɑ , l a ɾ ˈu ʃ k ə $wild vine, type of grape, clematis. Orel 2000: 111 as lërushkë. Vatasescu 1997(: 171) as larushk and larushkë, as are the forms also in Newmark 1998(:440), so we're going with that. The masculine larushk refers to wild grapevine (Vitis sylvestri) or dark grape vine used to make raki, the feminine larushkë refers to a mutlicolored cows. The feminine is counted as the inherited one here, also in light of Romanian lăuruscă. Bonnet 1998 also has lërushkë.

l ˈeː ɡ e m , l ˈi ɟ $law. Orel 2000(:31-2)ː Latin ē > i under the influence of following -i- "of the oblique cases (??). Orel 1998: notes variant singular forms ligjë, ligje; he treats it as reflecting a borrowing from Latin but takes note of Meyer-Lübke's (FEW: 11044) explanation for ē > i in "Sicilian dialectal mediation" (ancient or later?). Çabej 1967 (:381) instead sees ligj as analogized under the influence of a regular plural ligje, with the original sg/pl pair being legj/ligje; Bonnet 1998(: 64, 291-2) seems to favor this account, and later (Bonnet 1998: 127) he also voices suspicion that the regular singular perhaps should have even been *leg, the palatal also being due to the plural; p169 he states that ligj and grigj (< gregem) having final palatals should be due to the plural because of comparison to sagum > shag and fāgum > fag... however this leaves open the possibility that the vowel simply fell too early to palatalize the newly final consonant. 
l ˌe n t ˈɑː n i oː , l ə n d ˈɔ j $verb: to irritate, make painful. Orel 1998: 223-4. Bonnet attributes instead to laedere but does not explain; this is problematic, going with Orel. Vatasescu 1997(p55) does the same as Bonnet. 
l ˈe n t e m , l ˈə n d $lens. Analogical -ë in modern form? Or from a *lentā- perhaps even native? Lat. lens, lentis is a rival etymology for Cabej's view that it's from Lat. glans, coming from Bonnet 1998(: 249-50). 
l e ˈoː n e m , l ˈu a̯ n $lion. Final n may need explanation... ? Orel 1998: 232. Gheg luâ. Pl formsː luaj ~ luanj, luanë. In Buzuku, lua -- not expected luo; Gheg dialects also lack their respective lue and luu (Bonnet 1998: 76). The Latin etymology here is supported by Orel 1998(:232), Meyer 1891: 249, Çabej 1960, but Bonnet 1998 views the Gheg and Old Gheg data as seriously problematic to this view. Reexamine. Matasovic 2024: 64.

l ˌuː d ˈo n i oː , l ˈu a̯ j $verb: to play, wriggle. Gheg: luej. Aorist lojta (Gheg luejta), ppcl luar. Bonnet 1998: 288; Meyer 1891: 248; Mihaescu 1993: 46. For Bonnet 1998, a case of internal -d- loss. However a rival internal etymology supported by Çabej 1986 VII: 227, Camarda 1864: 50, Orël 1998: 232. This item is argued to be related also to loz and lodër by Çabej, and to an Old Irish luaidim "to celebrate"; Bonnet finds these arguments unconvincing but does remark that most Romance abandoned lūdēre for iocārī (with exception in Portuguese loir).  Lat inf. lūdere? Matasovic 2024: 64.

m ˌɑ n k ˈɑː n i oː , m u ŋ ɡ ˈɔ j $verb: to lack. Lat. inf. mancāre. Variant form: mëngoj. Orel 2000 p25. Also manca(n)tia > mungese? 
m ˈɑ n k i n u s , m ˈə ɲ ɟ ə ɾ $left. Orel 1998: 261; cf. Bonnet 1998: 293; Haarman 1972: 55, 134; Mihaescu 1993: 60, 66. 
m ˈɑ n k t u s , m ˈa j t ə $left hand. Orel 1998: 242. Orel 2000: 117. Gheg: shmajt, mâjtë; per LD -- Old Tosk mënk, MMA shmâkt. In theory, could just as well be from an Albanian cognate to Latin mancus, reflex of PIE mh2n-ko "maimed"; the same semantic development happening in parallel is possible though it may be more parsimonious to assume a Latin loan into Albanian so that a separate "maimed > left" development doesn't need to be assumed; but in either case the phonology that majtë reflects would be the same so it's not a meaningful difference. 
m ˌɑ n t ˈiː l e , m a n d ˈi l ɛ $kerchief, per Orel 2000: 57. This one is difficult. The Latin word had many forms, mantēle, mantēlium, maentellum, mantelum, mantīlium. Partially contaminated in Latin but Gaulish forms from mant-. But how do we explain the development here, without post-nasal voicing? We have the doublet of Byzantine Greek mandilia > Arabic mandil > Turkish  mendil > Albanian mindil (and Aromanian mandilă). Maybe Orel's right and it's regular, but it seems quite weird.
m ˌɑ n ˈu b r i u m , m ə ɾ ˈu $knife handle. Vatasescu 1997: 404. Bonnet 1998: 211 ~ Tosk definite is mëru-ri, plural mëru-rë-t, Gheg has mnuje and mënojë, which are possibly different treatments; Vatasescu also lists mënojë separately as its own feminine word meaning "handle", though they are synchronically equated in Newmark 1998(: 524). Bonnet notes also the hypercorrect Gheg forms m(ë)nû, m(ë)nuni, and mënonjë, and wonders about Byzantine influence on the Tosk form, noting also Cypriot menourin. 
m ˌɑ r ɡ ˈe l l ɑ , m a ɾ t͡s ˈɛ l $a gold women's origin. Romanian mărgea. Also Alb mercel, melcer. Cabej 1962: 197, Vatasescu 1997: 82. TODO may merit reexamination. 
m ˌɑ r ˈiː ɑ , m ə ɾ ˈi $Mary. Bonnet 1998: 391. 
m ˌɑː r i k ˈoː s u s , m ə ɾ k ˈɔ ʃ $"main lying in wife's bed after childbirth and recieving visitors". Orel 2000: 31. "Most recent layer of Latin borrowings" per Orel 2000(:33). 
m ˌɑ r i s l ˈuː k i u s , m l ˈy ʃ $pike (fish). As mlysh or mëlysh, another variant is mlyç. Orel 2000: 109; Orel 1998: 271. May be contaminated with mëlysh, mlysh "idiot", or that word may be drawn from it? Meyer 1891(: 284) noted Italian merluzzo "cod" but Dashi 2013 does not have this; that Italian word is itself attributed to Occitan merlus, which is attributed to Lat. merula, but phonologically this could reflect a borrowing from a West Romance doublet. Çabej 1986 (:I 352) and Bonnet 1998(: 88) list it under Latin lu:cius or lu:ceus. The final consonant outcome is eyebrow raising in any case, though mlyç is closer to the expected outcome of -q. Perhaps an Angevin loan (cf. Marr 2023, OF lus, luis "pike") might be considered. 
m ˌɑː r iː t ˈɑː n i oː , m a ɾ t ˈɔ j $verb: to marry. Orel 1998: 246. Bonnet 382.
m ˌɑː r iː t ˈɑː n i oː , m ə l t ˈɔ j $verb: to graft, to wed plants. martoj may also be used for wedding plants. Orel 1998: 259.

m ˈɑ r u m , m ˈa ɾ $strawberry. Alternativelyː bearberry, strawberry tree. Orel 1998: 245. Now mare out of plural tantum from original plural marë which was itself singularized. Alternate form marulë.
m ˈɑ r u n u s , m ə ɾ u $handle. Gheg: merû. Pl mërurë ~ mërun. Originally manurus in Romance, metathesized as entered Alb. Orel 1998(:263).
m ˈɑ s k u l u s , m ˈa ʃ k u lˠ $male. Attributed to nominative. Orel 2000: 55. Also the meaning hook from same word. Bonnet 382 as ma:sculus, but diff probably is trivial.

m ˌɑ s t ˈi kʰ i n u s , m ə ʃ t ˈɛ k ə ɾ $birch. Orel 2000: 56. Gheg mështekën. Bonnet 382 as mështker. 
m ˌɑ t ˈɑ k s u m , m ə n d ˈa f ʃ $silk. However, the Latin (and ultimate Greek) source is feminine. This may actually be native, just from a common root as Greek metaxa and Latin mataxa but not descended from them. Orel 2000: 118 etc.
m ˌɑː t r ˈiː k u s , m ə t ɾ ˈi k $tumor, intestinte gangrene in cattle. Orel 1998: 264. Bonnet 382.

m ˌɑ t t e ˈu k k ɑ m , m ə s ˈu k $shepherd's staff, threshing device or piece of wood used to crush knots in thread of linen or cotton. Variant "msuk". Romanian măciucă. Vatasescu 1997: 386.  
m ˌe d i k ˈɑː n i oː , m ə k ˈɔ j $verb: to feed, nurture. Orel 1998: 258. Vatasescu 1997: 43 -- notes this may have been interpreted (or even (re-)formed) in Albanian via më + koj (koj < dëkoj). She does also have the -ə-. Not in Balkan Romance. Matasovic 2024: 64. 
m ˌe d i k ˈɑ s t r u m , m ə ɡ ˈa ʃ t ə ɾ $sage. Orel 1998: 258. Variant mugashtër. Fem. Bonnet 382. Vatasescu 1997 variously has megashtër (p109) and mëgashtër (183). Not in Balkan Romance.
m ˈe d i k u m , m j ˈɛ k $doctor. Orel 1998: 268. Bonnet 382. De Vaan 2017: 1735.
m ˌe d i t ˈɑː n i oː , m ɛ j t ˈɔ j $verb: to meditate. Bonnet 306. Latin is cited originally for meditārī, but meditāre is also attested, and the verb often becomes active in Romance (Bonnet 306).
m ˈe d i u s , m ˈɛ s $middle. De Vaan 2017(ː 1742) takes this as a regular result; however Bonnet 1998(ː 156) finds it secondary, as result of reduction -- monophthongization -- due to its frequent use as a preposition (p55), prefering the mjez- of mjezditë as the regular outcome. Çabej 1960 meanwhile prefers an internal etymology. Orël 1998(: 271) is in agreement with Bonnet 1998 for mjez- as regular. 
m ˈe d i u s , m j ˈɛ z $mid-, as in mjezditë "midday", per Bonnet 1998(: 55,156); forms mjesditë and mesditë are under the influence of the reduced preposition mes. See above, and also for other views (i.e. De Vaan 2018; Çabej 1960).

m ˌe l ˈe t t ɑ m , b l ˈɛ t ə $bee, swarm, hive. Orel 2000: 25, 113. Earlier mbletë. May be native instead esp given religious importance of bees to Albanian folk culture. Source would be melettum, not feminine.

m ˈe n t ɑ , m ˈɛ n d ə $mint. Now mendër with suffix. Original form *mendë unattested. Orel 1998: 256. 
m ˈe n t e m , m ˈɛ n d $mind. Orel 2000: 25. mentum or mentem. Orel 1998: 256. Bonnet 382.

m ˈe t u l ɑ , n ˈɛ t u lˠ $heath (to make brooms), heather, Aaron's rod/great mullein -- Verbascum thapsus (Newmark 1998: 563). = Romanian mătură? Vatasescu 1997: 184 as netullë -- going with form from Newmark.

m ˌiː r ˈɑː n i oː , m ə ɾ ˈɛ j $verb: to fascinate, charm. Now mediopassive merehem: to be fascinated, charmed. Original Albanian active form unattested. Orel 2000: 262. Bonnet 383. Bonnet (p306) notes remarkableness in Albanian preserving the media tantum lost typically in Romance for such words.
m ˈi s s ɑ , m ˈɛ ʃ ə $mass (church). Orel 2000(:28-9) attributes it to "later layer of Latin loanwords" when short e reappeared. Bonnet 383. De Vaan 2017: 1735.

n ˌɑː t ˈuː r ɑ m , n a t ˈy ɾ ə $nature. Orel 2000: 30. Lack of double rr is not suspicious as yrë is a regular outcomeː cf detyrë, fytyrë, shëmbëlltyrë, yndyrë, vetyrë. n o d ˈu n k u l u s , n ˈə ɲ ɟ $knot. Orel 1998: 291. Cites Meyer Lübke 1935 (MEYER-LUBKE Gr. Grundriß 2 1 1054).
n ˈoː d u s , n ˈy e̯ $knot. Modern form is now nyjë, fem, singularized plural of nyjë, the plural of nye. Orel 2000: 55 etc. Vatasescu 1997(: 36) lists a feminine Gheg nye and nue vs. Tosk ne, nejë (which is masculine); Vatasescu indicates nyje too as a Gheg form. Landi apud Vatasescu argues that this Tosk ne is from nodus, the Latin singular, and nejë is from a Latin noda. She refers to Demiraj 1985 (pp133-4) "pentru dificultățile refericitoare la evoluția fonetică a lui NODUS...", and furthermore cites Bardhi's adjective "nyesh" as from a Latin NODOSVS. TODO consider adding that, or move this to a Gheg version of this lexicon?

n ˌo m i n ˈɑː n i oː , n u m ə ɾ ˈɔ j $to name, recite (different from numëroj < numerare). Cf the separate east Romance etyma for this , incl Old Romanian a număra, Aromanian numirare etc. Later confounded into a single term. n ˌu m e r ˈɑː n i oː , n u m ə ɾ ˈɔ j $verb: to count. May be later coinage based on numër.  Variant -- nëmëroj, cf Orel 2000: 30 cites this as actually be the regular one -- reexamine. Vatasescu 1997: 229. 
n ˈu m e r u m , n ˈu m ə ɾ $number. Bonnet 1998: 391 -- listed as "incertain Latin origin", but it seems to work. Vatasescu 1997: 229. 
n ˈuː p t i ɑ m , n ˈu s ɛ $spouse (f.). Archaic form nase. "Later Latin loan" per Orel 2000(:34) because Lat ū > Alb u. But could instead be native from a Proto-Albanian *nutjā < PIE *(s)nubʰ-tyeh2, or P Alb *snutjā < PIE snu-eh2 "daughter in law". Seems like a good candidate for exclusion. TODO reconsider. 
ˌo b l ˈɑː t ɑ m , b l ˈa t ə $wafer. Orel 1998; Orel 2000: 30. Bonnet 383 as mblatë, likewise Vatasescu p463; no Vlacho-Romance form listed. 
ˌo f ˈe l l ɑ m , f ˈɛ l ə $slice. Variant thelë. Vatasescu 1997: 229. No direct Romanian form per Vatasescu p229, with Rom. felie coming from Neo-Greek, although it is inherited in Dalmatian and some Italo-Romance dialects. 
ˌo l e ˈɑ s t e r , u lˠ ˈa ʃ t ə ɾ $olive tree. Orel 1998(:485), following Çabej 1986(II: 244-5) argues that the variant ullastër is from Italian olivastro "influenced by ulli". I would note Old Occitan ullastre. Orel 1998(:485) however thinks the form ullaSHtër is from Latin oleaster. Çabej earlier (1960) preferred a native suffixation upon ulli "olive" in -shtër (-a-?), while Bonnet 1998(: 223) instead repackages this as a calque on the Italian. Note influence from an existing ullashtër onto the loan of Italian olivastro is also possible. Vatasescu 1997(:163) has ullashtër frm olivaster, which would be inherited only in Alb. TODO consider using this one.
ˈo l e ɑ s t e r , v ˈa ʃ t ə ɾ $Ligustrum vulgare. Only attested as Gheg voshtër. Orel 2000: 37 -- part of a set of late Lat loans with init o > Tosk va ~ Gheg vo (<ve). Orel 1998: 514. Vatasescu 1997: 162. No Romanian reflex, but oleaster does have reflexes in Sardinian and Catalan apparently (Vatasescu 1997: 163). 
ˈo l e u m , v ˈa j $oil. May be native from a "mediterranean substrate" instead. See Schumacher and Matzinger 2013: 245. Arberesh vahj, Cham valë, Gheg voj -- seem to point more toward Latin via a Late Proto-Albanian *u̯ɔλa. Orel 2000(ː37) it's in a gropu of Later Lat loans where initial o > Tosk va ~ Geg vo (<ve). Gheg variant voj. Orel 1998: 492. Bonnet 383. De Vaan 2017: 1736. Arbereshe has val, some Gheg has vol, Lab/Cham val (Giordano 1963, JOkl 1923 p210, Pekmezi 1908 p63 respectively, all apud Vatasescu 1997 p90).
ˌo l ˈiː w u s , u lˠ ˈi $olive, olive tree. Arvanitika: lli. Gheg: ullî /ulˠĩ/, nasalized, -n is secondary. Orel 2000: 55. Articulated forms ulliri (Tosk), ullini (Gheg) lead Kristophson (1988: 77) to propose an *olivanus as the source -- tested below -- but Cabej 1967(:78) and Demiraj 1985(:172) apud Vatasescu 1967 (: 168) argue these definite forms are the result of analogical extension of the pattern seen in molīnum > mulli/mullĩ, def. form mullini. Not inherited in Romanian, but found in Dalmatian, Italian and Sardinian. TODO check for nasality in Gheg. 
ˌo l iː w ˈɑː n u s , u lˠ ˈi $olive, olive tree, per theory of Kristophson 1988 -- see above. Suffixation with attributional -ānus; requires stress shift at some point. 
ˈo p e r ɑ , v ˈɛ p ə ɾ $deed, work (art). May be from (native) epër instead. But Orel 2000: 36 -- pre LPA. Orel 1998(ː499) attributes to unattested vapër ~ vopër, with vepër being a singularized plural. However, per Vatasescu 1997(ː327), Old Albanian has vepër too, so we're using this. 
ˌo p e r ˈɑː n i oː , v ɛ p ɾ ˈɔ j $to put into action, do, work, take effect. Per Vatasescu 1997(:327), "nu este excluse derivarea de la vepër". 
ˌoː r ˈɑː n i oː , u ɾ ˈɔ j $verb: to congratulate, wish, elicitate. Cf Romanian a urâ, but re-examine. Topalli 2017: 1533. Orel 1998: 469. Vatasescu 1997: 464. Aromanian or, aor, uredzu. Matasovic 2024: 64. 
ˌo r b ˈɑː n u s , v ˈɛ ɾ b ə ɾ $blind. Cf Dalmatian vuarb, Romanian orb. Orbanus form from Schumacher and Matzinger 2013: 225. Earlier Lat loan i.e. pre-LPA per Orel 2000: 36. Some assert a Lat ō. Orel 1998: 499. De Vaan 2017: 1744 as "blind person". Pre June 2024 this was ˈorbanus, but the suffix should be -ānus so this is fixed. Stress placement confirmed per native speakers. 
ˈo r b u s , v ˈɛ ɾ b $blind. Earlier Lat loan i.e. pre LPA per Orel 2000: 36. Some assert a Lat ō. Later in -ër. Bonnet 383ː on -ër and -ët, 40 and 344. 
ˌoː r d i n ˈɑː n i oː , u ɾ ð ə ɾ ˈɔ j $verb: to command, to give an order. Old Romanian a urdina, Meglenite urdin. Old Albanian me urdhënuem in Bogdiani and Kuvendi ii Arbënit, derived urdhëratë. Haarmann 1972(:27 ) argued this is derived from urdhër, but others do not. Vatasescu 1997: 433. 
ˌoː r d i n ˈɑː t ɑ m , u ɾ ð ə ɾ ˈa t ə $servant. A Tosk item. Cabej 1986(:250) derives this from urdhër or urdhëroj, with Orel 1998(:488) following him, but this could easily be regular and/or analogized on the basis of those words, as ōrdinātus,-a,-um is indeed present in Latin. (THIS IS A PROPOSAL.)
ˈoː r d i n e m , ˈu ɾ ð ə ɾ $order. Long o per Orel 2000(ː33) but development in Romance suggested it came from short o (cf French ordre etc). Orel 1998: 488. Bonnet 383. De Vaan 2017: 1735. Old Gheg urdhënë in Bardhi -- Vatasescu 1997: 433. 
ˈo r n u m , v a r $grave. From Latin urna "cinerary urn". Diefenbach and Çabej attribute instead to Greek ornxw. Meyer to IE wer- "to wrap. Reexamine... In any case, Gheg vorr. Feminized plural in -e. Orel 1998: 495. 
ˈo r pʰ ɑ n u s , v ˈa ɾ f ə ɾ $poor. Semantic development shared with Aromanian oarfân and Romanian oarfăn.  Orel 2000(ː37) it's in a group of Later Lat loans where initial o > Tosk va ~ Geg vo (<ve). Gheg vorfën. Orel 1998: 495. Bonnet 383. De Vaan 2017: 1736. Mann 1948 p546 & 563 and Vatasescu 1997 p113: Old Gheg vorfën

ˈoː t u s , ˈu t $owl. Orel 2000: 33. Orel 1998: 491. Variant hut.
